<p><b>Dante Alighieri</b> (<small>Italian:¬†</small><span class="IPA" lang="it-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Italian" title="Help:IPA/Italian">[Ààdante aliÀà…°j…õÀêri]</a></span>), probably baptized <b>Durante di Alighiero degli Alighieri</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[note 1]</a></sup> and often referred to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mononymous_person" title="Mononymous person">simply</a> as <b>Dante</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Àà/: primary stress follows">Àà</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/…ëÀê/: 'a' in 'father'">…ëÀê</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/e…™/: 'a' in 'face'">e…™</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Àà/: primary stress follows">Àà</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/√¶/: 'a' in 'bad'">√¶</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/e…™/: 'a' in 'face'">e…™</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Àà/: primary stress follows">Àà</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/√¶/: 'a' in 'bad'">√¶</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[3]</a></sup> <small>also</small> <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><small><a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">US</a>: </small><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/Àà/: primary stress follows">Àà</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/…ëÀê/: 'a' in 'father'">…ëÀê</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup> <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">‚Äâ1265</span> ‚Äì 14 September 1321), was an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_people" title="Italian people">Italian</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[a]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Italian_poetry" title="Italian poetry">poet</a>, writer and philosopher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[6]</a></sup> His <i><a href="/wiki/Divine_Comedy" title="Divine Comedy">Divine Comedy</a></i>, originally called <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">Comed√¨a</i></span> (modern Italian: <i>Commedia</i>) and later christened <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">Divina</i></span> by <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Boccaccio" title="Giovanni Boccaccio">Giovanni Boccaccio</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[7]</a></sup> is widely considered one of the most important poems of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> and the greatest literary work in the Italian language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dante is known for establishing the use of the <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernacular</a> in literature at a time when most poetry was written in <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, which was accessible only to the most educated readers. His <i><a href="/wiki/De_vulgari_eloquentia" title="De vulgari eloquentia">De vulgari eloquentia</a></i> (<i>On Eloquence in the Vernacular</i>) was one of the first scholarly defenses of the vernacular. His use of the <a href="/wiki/Florentine_dialect" title="Florentine dialect">Florentine dialect</a> for works such as <i><a href="/wiki/La_Vita_Nuova" title="La Vita Nuova">The New Life</a></i> (1295) and <i>Divine Comedy</i> helped establish the modern-day standardized Italian language. His work set a precedent that important Italian writers such as <a href="/wiki/Petrarch" title="Petrarch">Petrarch</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boccaccio" title="Boccaccio">Boccaccio</a> would later follow.
</p><p>Dante was instrumental in establishing the literature of Italy. His depictions of <a href="/wiki/Hell" title="Hell">Hell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Purgatory" title="Purgatory">Purgatory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heaven" title="Heaven">Heaven</a> provided inspiration for the larger body of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_art" title="Western art">Western art</a> and literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[11]</a></sup> He is cited as an influence on such English writers as <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">Geoffrey Chaucer</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Milton" title="John Milton">John Milton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alfred,_Lord_Tennyson" title="Alfred, Lord Tennyson">Alfred Tennyson</a>, among many others. In addition, the first use of the interlocking three-line rhyme scheme, or the <i><a href="/wiki/Terza_rima" title="Terza rima">terza rima</a></i>, is attributed to him. He is described as the "father" of the Italian language,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[12]</a></sup> and in Italy he is often referred to as <i><span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">il Sommo Poeta</i></span></i> ("the Supreme Poet"). Dante, Petrarch, and Boccaccio are also called the <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">tre corone</i></span> ("three crowns") of Italian literature.
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dante_Alighieri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Dante was born in <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Florence" title="Republic of Florence">Republic of Florence</a>, in what is now Italy. The exact date of his birth is unknown, although it is generally believed to be around 1265.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dbi_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-dbi-15">[13]</a></sup> This can be deduced from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autobiographical" title="Autobiographical">autobiographic</a> <a href="/wiki/Allusion" title="Allusion">allusions</a> in the <i><a href="/wiki/Divine_Comedy" title="Divine Comedy">Divine Comedy</a>.</i> Its first section, the <i><a href="/wiki/Inferno_(Dante)" title="Inferno (Dante)">Inferno</a>,</i> begins, <i>"Nel mezzo del cammin di nostra vita"</i> ("Midway upon the journey of our life"), implying that Dante was around 35 years old, since the average lifespan according to the Bible (Psalm 89:10, Vulgate) is 70 years; and since his imaginary travel to the netherworld took place in 1300, he was most probably born around 1265. Some verses of the <i>Paradiso</i> section of the <i>Divine Comedy</i> also provide a possible clue that he was born under the sign of <a href="/wiki/Gemini_(astrology)" title="Gemini (astrology)">Gemini</a>: "As I revolved with the eternal twins, I saw revealed, from hills to river outlets, the threshing-floor that makes us so ferocious" (XXII ¬†151‚Äì154). In 1265, the sun was in Gemini between approximately 11 May and 11 June (<a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian calendar</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dante-alighieri.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="303" data-file-width="239" decoding="async" height="279" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Dante-alighieri.jpg/220px-Dante-alighieri.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a4/Dante-alighieri.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dante-alighieri.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Dante Alighieri</i>, attributed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giotto_di_Bondone" title="Giotto di Bondone">Giotto</a>, in the chapel of the <a href="/wiki/Bargello" title="Bargello">Bargello</a> palace in Florence. This oldest picture of Dante was painted just prior to his exile and has since been extensively restored.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dante_alighieri,_Palazzo_dei_Giudici.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1084" data-file-width="936" decoding="async" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Dante_alighieri%2C_Palazzo_dei_Giudici.jpg/170px-Dante_alighieri%2C_Palazzo_dei_Giudici.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Dante_alighieri%2C_Palazzo_dei_Giudici.jpg/255px-Dante_alighieri%2C_Palazzo_dei_Giudici.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Dante_alighieri%2C_Palazzo_dei_Giudici.jpg/340px-Dante_alighieri%2C_Palazzo_dei_Giudici.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dante_alighieri,_Palazzo_dei_Giudici.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of Dante, from a fresco in the Palazzo dei Giudici, Florence</div></div></div>
<p>Dante claimed that his family descended from the ancient Romans (<i>Inferno</i>, XV, 76), but the earliest relative he could mention by name was <a href="/wiki/Cacciaguida" title="Cacciaguida">Cacciaguida</a> degli Elisei (<i>Paradiso</i>, XV, 135), born no earlier than about 1100. Dante's father, <a href="/wiki/Alighiero_di_Bellincione" title="Alighiero di Bellincione">Alighiero di Bellincione</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trecc_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trecc-17">[15]</a></sup> was a White <a href="/wiki/Guelphs_and_Ghibellines" title="Guelphs and Ghibellines">Guelph</a> who suffered no reprisals after the <a href="/wiki/Guelphs_and_Ghibellines" title="Guelphs and Ghibellines">Ghibellines</a> won the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Montaperti" title="Battle of Montaperti">Battle of Montaperti</a> in the middle of the 13th¬†century. This suggests that Alighiero or his family may have enjoyed some protective prestige and status, although some suggest that the politically inactive Alighiero was of such low standing that he was not considered worth exiling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dante's family was loyal to the Guelphs, a political alliance that supported the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papacy" title="Papacy">Papacy</a> and that was involved in complex opposition to the Ghibellines, who were backed by the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperor</a>. The poet's mother was Bella, probably a member of the Abati family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trecc_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Trecc-17">[15]</a></sup> She died when Dante was not yet ten years old. His father Alighiero soon married again, to Lapa di Chiarissimo Cialuffi. It is uncertain whether he really married her, since widowers were socially limited in such matters, but she definitely bore him two children, Dante's half-brother Francesco and half-sister Tana (Gaetana).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trecc_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Trecc-17">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dante said he first met <a href="/wiki/Beatrice_Portinari" title="Beatrice Portinari">Beatrice Portinari</a>, daughter of <a href="/wiki/Folco_Portinari" title="Folco Portinari">Folco Portinari</a>, when he was nine (she was eight),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[17]</a></sup> and he claimed to have fallen in love with her "<a href="/wiki/Love_at_first_sight" title="Love at first sight">at first sight</a>", apparently without even talking with her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[18]</a></sup> When he was 12, however, he was promised in marriage to <a href="/wiki/Gemma_Donati" title="Gemma Donati">Gemma di Manetto Donati</a>, daughter of Manetto Donati, member of the powerful Donati family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trecc_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-Trecc-17">[15]</a></sup> Contracting marriages for children at such an early age was quite common and involved a formal ceremony, including contracts signed before a <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_notary" title="Civil law notary">notary</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trecc_17-4"><a href="#cite_note-Trecc-17">[15]</a></sup> Dante claimed to have seen Beatrice again frequently after he turned 18, exchanging greetings with her in the streets of Florence, though he never knew her well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jmdent_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-jmdent-21">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Years after his marriage to Gemma, he claims to have met Beatrice again; he wrote several sonnets to Beatrice but never mentioned Gemma in any of his poems. He refers to other Donati relations, notably Forese and Piccarda, in his <i>Divine Comedy.</i> The exact date of his marriage is not known; the only certain information is that, before his exile in 1301, he had fathered three children with Gemma (Pietro, <a href="/wiki/Jacopo_Alighieri" title="Jacopo Alighieri">Jacopo</a> and Antonia).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trecc_17-5"><a href="#cite_note-Trecc-17">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dante fought with the Guelph cavalry at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Campaldino" title="Battle of Campaldino">Battle of Campaldino</a> (11 June, 1289).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davenport2005_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davenport2005-22">[20]</a></sup> This victory brought about a reformation of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Florence" title="Republic of Florence">Florentine</a> constitution. To take part in public life, one had to enroll in one of the city's many commercial or artisan guilds, so Dante entered the Physicians' and Apothecaries' Guild. In the following years, his name is occasionally recorded as speaking or voting in the various councils of the republic. A substantial portion of minutes from such meetings in the years 1298‚Äì1300 was lost, however, so the true extent of Dante's participation in the city's councils is uncertain.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education_and_poetry">Education and poetry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dante_Alighieri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Education and poetry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DanteFresco.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3661" data-file-width="2072" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/DanteFresco.jpg/170px-DanteFresco.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/DanteFresco.jpg/255px-DanteFresco.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/DanteFresco.jpg/340px-DanteFresco.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DanteFresco.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mural of Dante in the <a href="/wiki/Uffizi" title="Uffizi">Uffizi</a>, Florence, by <a href="/wiki/Andrea_del_Castagno" title="Andrea del Castagno">Andrea del Castagno</a>, c. 1450</div></div></div>
<p>Not much is known about Dante's education; he presumably studied at home or in a chapter school attached to a church or monastery in Florence. It is known that he studied <a href="/wiki/Tuscany" title="Tuscany">Tuscan</a> poetry and that he admired the compositions of the Bolognese poet <a href="/wiki/Guido_Guinizelli" title="Guido Guinizelli">Guido Guinizelli</a>‚Äîin <i>Purgatorio</i> XXVI he characterized him as his "father"‚Äîat a time when the <a href="/wiki/Sicilian_School" title="Sicilian School">Sicilian School</a> (<i>Scuola poetica Siciliana</i>), a cultural group from <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>, was becoming known in Tuscany. He also discovered the <a href="/wiki/Occitan_language" title="Occitan language">Proven√ßal</a> poetry of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Troubadours" title="Troubadours">troubadours</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Arnaut_Daniel" title="Arnaut Daniel">Arnaut Daniel</a>, and the Latin writers of <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a> and especially <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dante's interactions with Beatrice set an example of so-called <a href="/wiki/Courtly_love" title="Courtly love">courtly love</a>, a phenomenon developed in French and Proven√ßal poetry of prior centuries. Dante's experience of such love was typical, but his expression of it was unique. It was in the name of this love that Dante left his imprint on the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dolce_stil_novo" title="Dolce stil novo">dolce stil novo</a></i> ("sweet new style", a term that Dante himself coined), and he would join other contemporary poets and writers in exploring never-before-emphasized aspects of love (<i>Amore</i>). Love for Beatrice (as <a href="/wiki/Petrarch" title="Petrarch">Petrarch</a> would express for Laura somewhat differently) would be his reason for writing poetry and for living, together with political passions. In many of his poems, she is depicted as semi-divine, watching over him constantly and providing spiritual instruction, sometimes harshly. When Beatrice died in 1290, Dante sought refuge in Latin literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[22]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Convivio" title="Convivio">Convivio</a></i> chronicles his having read <a href="/wiki/Boethius" title="Boethius">Boethius</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Consolation_of_Philosophy" title="The Consolation of Philosophy">De consolatione philosophiae</a></i> and Cicero's <i><a href="/wiki/Laelius_de_Amicitia" title="Laelius de Amicitia">De Amicitia</a>.</i>
</p><p>He next dedicated himself to philosophical studies at religious schools like the Dominican one in <a href="/wiki/Santa_Maria_Novella" title="Santa Maria Novella">Santa Maria Novella</a>. He took part in the disputes that the two principal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mendicant_order" title="Mendicant order">mendicant</a> orders (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franciscan" title="Franciscan">Franciscan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominican</a>) publicly or indirectly held in Florence, the former explaining the doctrines of the mystics and of St. <a href="/wiki/Bonaventure" title="Bonaventure">Bonaventure</a>, the latter expounding on the theories of St. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jmdent_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-jmdent-21">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>At 18, Dante met <a href="/wiki/Guido_Cavalcanti" title="Guido Cavalcanti">Guido Cavalcanti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lapo_Gianni" title="Lapo Gianni">Lapo Gianni</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cino_da_Pistoia" title="Cino da Pistoia">Cino da Pistoia</a> and, soon after, <a href="/wiki/Brunetto_Latini" title="Brunetto Latini">Brunetto Latini</a>; together they became the leaders of the <i>dolce stil novo.</i> Brunetto later received special mention in the <i>Divine Comedy</i> (<i>Inferno</i>, XV, 28) for what he had taught Dante: <i>Nor speaking less on that account I go With Ser Brunetto, and I ask who are his most known and most eminent companions.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup> Some fifty poetical commentaries by Dante are known (the so-called <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Rime" title="The Rime">Rime</a>,</i> rhymes), others being included in the later <i>Vita Nuova</i> and <i>Convivio.</i> Other studies are reported, or deduced from <i>Vita Nuova</i> or the <i>Comedy,</i> regarding painting and music.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Florence_and_politics">Florence and politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dante_Alighieri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Florence and politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Guelphs_and_Ghibellines" title="Guelphs and Ghibellines">Guelphs and Ghibellines</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Dante_Alighieri_(Uffizi).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6398" data-file-width="3844" decoding="async" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Statue_of_Dante_Alighieri_%28Uffizi%29.jpg/170px-Statue_of_Dante_Alighieri_%28Uffizi%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Statue_of_Dante_Alighieri_%28Uffizi%29.jpg/255px-Statue_of_Dante_Alighieri_%28Uffizi%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Statue_of_Dante_Alighieri_%28Uffizi%29.jpg/340px-Statue_of_Dante_Alighieri_%28Uffizi%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Dante_Alighieri_(Uffizi).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Dante at the Uffizi</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dante_Alighieri_Florence_Firenze_JBU01.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3959" data-file-width="2969" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Dante_Alighieri_Florence_Firenze_JBU01.JPG/170px-Dante_Alighieri_Florence_Firenze_JBU01.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Dante_Alighieri_Florence_Firenze_JBU01.JPG/255px-Dante_Alighieri_Florence_Firenze_JBU01.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Dante_Alighieri_Florence_Firenze_JBU01.JPG/340px-Dante_Alighieri_Florence_Firenze_JBU01.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dante_Alighieri_Florence_Firenze_JBU01.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Monument_to_Dante" title="Monument to Dante">Statue of Dante</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Piazza_Santa_Croce" title="Piazza Santa Croce">Piazza Santa Croce</a> in Florence, <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Pazzi" title="Enrico Pazzi">Enrico Pazzi</a>, 1865</div></div></div>
<p>Dante, like most Florentines of his day, was embroiled in the <a href="/wiki/Guelphs_and_Ghibellines" title="Guelphs and Ghibellines">Guelph‚ÄìGhibelline conflict</a>. He fought in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Campaldino" title="Battle of Campaldino">Battle of Campaldino</a> (11 June, 1289), with the Florentine Guelphs against <a href="/wiki/Arezzo" title="Arezzo">Arezzo</a> Ghibellines;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davenport2005_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Davenport2005-22">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DASM_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-DASM-26">[24]</a></sup> then in 1294 he was among the escorts of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Martel_of_Anjou" title="Charles Martel of Anjou">Charles Martel of Anjou</a> (grandson of <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_Anjou" title="Charles I of Anjou">Charles¬†I of Anjou</a>) while he was in Florence. To further his political career, he became a pharmacist. He did not intend to practice as one, but a law issued in 1295 required nobles aspiring to public office to be enrolled in one of the Corporazioni delle Arti e dei Mestieri, so Dante obtained admission to the Apothecaries' Guild. This profession was not inappropriate, since at that time books were sold from apothecaries' shops. As a politician, he accomplished little but held various offices over some years in a city rife with political unrest.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>After defeating the Ghibellines, the Guelphs divided into two factions: the White Guelphs (<i>Guelfi Bianchi</i>)‚ÄîDante's party, led by Vieri dei Cerchi‚Äîand the Black Guelphs (<i>Guelfi Neri</i>), led by <a href="/wiki/Corso_Donati" title="Corso Donati">Corso Donati</a>. Although the split was along family lines at first, ideological differences arose based on opposing views of the papal role in Florentine affairs. The Blacks supported the Pope and the Whites wanted more freedom from Rome. The Whites took power first and expelled the Blacks. In response, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Boniface_VIII" title="Pope Boniface VIII">Pope Boniface VIII</a> planned a military occupation of Florence. In 1301, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_of_Valois" title="Charles of Valois">Charles of Valois</a>, brother of King <a href="/wiki/Philip_IV_of_France" title="Philip IV of France">Philip IV of France</a>, was expected to visit Florence because the Pope had appointed him as peacemaker for <a href="/wiki/Tuscany" title="Tuscany">Tuscany</a>. But the city's government had treated the Pope's ambassadors badly a few weeks before, seeking independence from papal influence. It was believed that Charles had received other unofficial instructions, so the council sent a delegation that included Dante to Rome to ascertain the Pope's intentions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Exile_from_Florence">Exile from Florence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dante_Alighieri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Exile from Florence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Pope Boniface quickly dismissed the other delegates and asked Dante alone to remain in Rome. At the same time (1 November, 1301), Charles of Valois entered Florence with the Black Guelphs, who in the next six days destroyed much of the city and killed many of their enemies. A new Black Guelph government was installed, and <a href="/wiki/Cante_dei_Gabrielli" title="Cante dei Gabrielli">Cante dei Gabrielli</a> da <a href="/wiki/Gubbio" title="Gubbio">Gubbio</a> was appointed <i><a href="/wiki/Podest%C3%A0" title="Podest√†">podest√†</a></i> of the city. In March 1302, Dante, a White Guelph by affiliation, along with the <a href="/wiki/Gherardini_family" title="Gherardini family">Gherardini family</a>, was condemned to exile for two years and ordered to pay a large fine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup> Dante was accused of corruption and financial wrongdoing by the Black Guelphs for the time that Dante was serving as city prior (Florence's highest position) for two months in 1300.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup> The poet was still in Rome in 1302, as the Pope, who had backed the Black Guelphs, had "suggested" that Dante stay there. Florence under the Black Guelphs, therefore, considered Dante an absconder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dante did not pay the fine, in part because he believed he was not guilty and in part because all his assets in Florence had been seized by the Black Guelphs. He was condemned to perpetual exile; if he had returned to Florence without paying the fine, he could have been burned at the stake. (In June 2008, nearly seven centuries after his death, the city council of Florence passed a motion rescinding Dante's sentence.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[28]</a></sup> In 1306‚Äì07, Dante was a guest of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Moroello_Malaspina&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Moroello Malaspina (page does not exist)">Moroello Malaspina</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">¬†[<a class="extiw" href="https://it.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moroello_Malaspina" title="it:Moroello Malaspina">it</a>]</span> in the region of <a href="/wiki/Lunigiana" title="Lunigiana">Lunigiana</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaffa202024_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaffa202024-31">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antonio_Cotti_-_Dante_a_Verona.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1803" data-file-width="2126" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Antonio_Cotti_-_Dante_a_Verona.jpg/220px-Antonio_Cotti_-_Dante_a_Verona.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Antonio_Cotti_-_Dante_a_Verona.jpg/330px-Antonio_Cotti_-_Dante_a_Verona.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Antonio_Cotti_-_Dante_a_Verona.jpg/440px-Antonio_Cotti_-_Dante_a_Verona.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antonio_Cotti_-_Dante_a_Verona.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Dante in Verona</i>, by Antonio Cotti</div></div></div>
<p>Dante took part in several attempts by the White Guelphs to regain power, but these failed due to treachery. Bitter at the treatment he received from his enemies, he grew disgusted with the infighting and ineffectiveness of his erstwhile allies and vowed to become a party of one. He went to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Verona,_Italy" title="Verona, Italy">Verona</a> as a guest of <a href="/wiki/Bartolomeo_I_della_Scala" title="Bartolomeo I della Scala">Bartolomeo I della Scala</a>, then moved to <a href="/wiki/Sarzana" title="Sarzana">Sarzana</a> in Liguria. Later he is supposed to have lived in <a href="/wiki/Lucca" title="Lucca">Lucca</a> with a woman named Gentucca. She apparently made his stay comfortable (and he later gratefully mentioned her in <i>Purgatorio</i>, XXIV, 37). Some speculative sources claim he visited <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a> between 1308 and 1310, and other sources even less trustworthy say he went to <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a>: these claims, first made in <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Boccaccio" title="Giovanni Boccaccio">Boccaccio</a>'s book on Dante several decades after his death, seem inspired by readers who were impressed with the poet's wide learning and erudition. Evidently, Dante's command of philosophy and his literary interests deepened in exile and when he was no longer busy with the day-to-day business of Florentine domestic politics, and this is evidenced in his prose writings in this period. There is no real evidence that he ever left Italy. Dante's <i>Immensa Dei dilectione testante</i> to Henry VII of Luxembourg confirms his residence "beneath the springs of Arno, near Tuscany" in March 1311.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In 1310, Holy Roman Emperor <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry VII, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry VII</a> of <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a> marched into Italy at the head of 5,000 troops. Dante saw in him a new <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a> who would restore the office of the Holy Roman Emperor to its former glory and also retake Florence from the Black Guelphs. He wrote to Henry and several Italian princes, demanding that they destroy the Black Guelphs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup> Mixing religion and private concerns in his writings, he invoked the worst anger of God against his city and suggested several particular targets, who were also his personal enemies. It was during this time that he wrote <i><a href="/wiki/De_Monarchia" title="De Monarchia">De Monarchia</a></i>, proposing a <a href="/wiki/Universal_monarchy" title="Universal monarchy">universal monarchy</a> under Henry VII.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>At some point during his exile, he conceived of the <i>Comedy</i>, but the date is uncertain. The work is much more assured and on a larger scale than anything he had written in Florence; it is likely he would have undertaken such a work only after he realized his political ambitions, which had been central to him up to his banishment, had been halted for some time, possibly forever. It is also noticeable that Beatrice has returned to his imagination with renewed force and with a wider meaning than in the <i>Vita Nuova</i>; in <i>Convivio</i> (written c. 1304‚Äì07) he had declared that the memory of this youthful romance belonged to the past.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>An early indication that the poem was underway is a notice by <a href="/wiki/Francesco_da_Barberino" title="Francesco da Barberino">Francesco da Barberino</a>, tucked into his <i>Documenti d'Amore</i> (<i>Lessons of Love</i>), probably written in 1314 or early 1315. Francesco notes that Dante followed the <i><a href="/wiki/Aeneid" title="Aeneid">Aeneid</a></i> in a poem called "Comedy" and that the setting of this poem (or part of it) was the underworld; i.e., hell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup> The brief note gives no incontestable indication that Barberino had seen or read even the <i>Inferno</i>, or that this part had been published at the time, but it indicates composition was well underway and that the sketching of the poem might have begun some years before. (It has been suggested that a knowledge of Dante's work also underlies some of the illuminations in Francesco da Barberino's earlier <i>Officiolum</i> [c. 1305‚Äì08], a manuscript that came to light in 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[34]</a></sup>) It is known that the <i>Inferno</i> had been published by 1317; this is established by quoted lines interspersed in the margins of contemporary dated records from <a href="/wiki/Bologna" title="Bologna">Bologna</a>, but there is no certainty as to whether the three parts of the poem were each published in full or, rather, a few cantos at a time. <i>Paradiso</i> seems to have been published posthumously.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Monument_to_Dante_(Verona).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="7000" data-file-width="4721" decoding="async" height="252" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Monument_to_Dante_%28Verona%29.jpg/170px-Monument_to_Dante_%28Verona%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Monument_to_Dante_%28Verona%29.jpg/255px-Monument_to_Dante_%28Verona%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Monument_to_Dante_%28Verona%29.jpg/340px-Monument_to_Dante_%28Verona%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Monument_to_Dante_(Verona).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Dante Alighieri in Verona</div></div></div>
<p>In 1312 Henry assaulted Florence and defeated the Black Guelphs, but there is no evidence that Dante was involved. Some say he refused to participate in the attack on his city by a foreigner; others suggest that he had become unpopular with the White Guelphs, too, and that any trace of his passage had carefully been removed. Henry VII died (from a fever) in 1313 and with him any hope for Dante to see Florence again. He returned to Verona, where <a href="/wiki/Cangrande_I_della_Scala" title="Cangrande I della Scala">Cangrande I della Scala</a> allowed him to live in certain security and, presumably, in a fair degree of prosperity. Cangrande was admitted to Dante's Paradise (<i>Paradiso</i>, XVII, 76).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the period of his exile, Dante corresponded with Dominican theologian Fr. <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nicholas_Brunacci&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nicholas Brunacci (page does not exist)">Nicholas Brunacci</a> OP [1240‚Äì1322], who had been a student of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a> at the Santa Sabina <i>studium</i> in Rome, later at Paris,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup> and of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albert_the_Great" title="Albert the Great">Albert the Great</a> at the Cologne <i>studium</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup> Brunacci became lector at the Santa Sabina <i>studium</i>, forerunner of the <a href="/wiki/Pontifical_University_of_Saint_Thomas_Aquinas" title="Pontifical University of Saint Thomas Aquinas">Pontifical University of Saint Thomas Aquinas</a>, and later served in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_curia" title="Roman curia">papal curia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1315, Florence was forced by <a href="/wiki/Uguccione_della_Faggiuola" title="Uguccione della Faggiuola">Uguccione della Faggiuola</a> (the military officer controlling the town) to grant an amnesty to those in exile, including Dante. But for this, Florence required public penance in addition to payment of a high fine. Dante refused, preferring to remain in exile. When Uguccione defeated Florence, Dante's death sentence was commuted to house arrest, on condition that he go to Florence to swear he would never enter the town again. He refused to go, and his death sentence was confirmed and extended to his sons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup> He still hoped late in life that he might be invited back to Florence on honorable terms.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_burial">Death and burial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dante_Alighieri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Death and burial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:328px;max-width:328px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:157px;max-width:157px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dante%27s_tomb_(Ravenna)_-_Facade.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="7694" data-file-width="4846" decoding="async" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Dante%27s_tomb_%28Ravenna%29_-_Facade.jpg/155px-Dante%27s_tomb_%28Ravenna%29_-_Facade.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Dante%27s_tomb_%28Ravenna%29_-_Facade.jpg/233px-Dante%27s_tomb_%28Ravenna%29_-_Facade.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Dante%27s_tomb_%28Ravenna%29_-_Facade.jpg/310px-Dante%27s_tomb_%28Ravenna%29_-_Facade.jpg 2x" width="155"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:167px;max-width:167px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dante_Alieghri_tomb_in_Ravenna_(interior).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="4496" data-file-width="3328" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Dante_Alieghri_tomb_in_Ravenna_%28interior%29.jpg/165px-Dante_Alieghri_tomb_in_Ravenna_%28interior%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Dante_Alieghri_tomb_in_Ravenna_%28interior%29.jpg/248px-Dante_Alieghri_tomb_in_Ravenna_%28interior%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Dante_Alieghri_tomb_in_Ravenna_%28interior%29.jpg/330px-Dante_Alieghri_tomb_in_Ravenna_%28interior%29.jpg 2x" width="165"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Dante's tomb exterior and interior in Ravenna, built in 1780</div></div></div></div>
<p>Dante's final days were spent in <a href="/wiki/Ravenna" title="Ravenna">Ravenna</a>, where he had been invited to stay in the city in 1318 by its prince, <a href="/wiki/Guido_II_da_Polenta" title="Guido II da Polenta">Guido II da Polenta</a>. Dante died in Ravenna on 14 September 1321, aged about 56, of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quartan_malaria" title="Quartan malaria">quartan malaria</a> contracted while returning from a diplomatic mission to the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Republic of Venice</a>. He was attended by his three children, and possibly by Gemma Donati, and by friends and admirers he had in the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaffa202023‚Äì24,_27,_28‚Äì30_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaffa202023‚Äì24,_27,_28‚Äì30-42">[40]</a></sup> He was buried in Ravenna at the Church of San Pier Maggiore (later called <a href="/wiki/Basilica_of_San_Francesco,_Ravenna" title="Basilica of San Francesco, Ravenna">Basilica di San Francesco</a>). <a href="/wiki/Bernardo_Bembo" title="Bernardo Bembo">Bernardo Bembo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Praetor" title="Praetor">praetor</a> of <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a>, erected <a href="/wiki/Tomb_of_Dante" title="Tomb of Dante">a tomb for him</a> in 1483.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the grave, a verse of <a href="/wiki/Bernardo_Canaccio" title="Bernardo Canaccio">Bernardo Canaccio</a>, a friend of Dante, is dedicated to Florence:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1109925960">.mw-parser-output .verse_translation .translated{padding-left:2em}@media only screen and (max-width:43.75em){.mw-parser-output .verse_translation.wrap_when_small td{display:block;padding-left:0}.mw-parser-output .verse_translation.wrap_when_small .translated{padding-left:0}}</style>
<table class="verse_translation wrap_when_small" role="presentation" style="margin-left:1em;">
<tbody><tr style="vertical-align: top;">
<td><div class="poem" lang="" style="font-style:italic;text-align:left">
<p><span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">parvi Florentia mater amoris</i></span>
</p>
</div>
</td>
<td class="translated"><div class="poem">
<p>Florence, mother of little love
</p>
</div>
</td></tr>
<tr style="display: none;">
<td>
</td>
<td style="padding-left: 2em;">
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In 1329, <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_du_Pouget" title="Bertrand du Pouget">Bertrand du Pouget</a>, Cardinal and nephew of <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_XXII" title="Pope John XXII">Pope John XXII</a>, classified Dante's <i>Monarchia</i> as heretical and sought to have his bones burned at the stake. <a href="/wiki/Ostasio_I_da_Polenta" title="Ostasio I da Polenta">Ostasio I da Polenta</a> and Pino della Tosa, allies of Pouget, interceded to prevent the destruction of Dante's remains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaffa202038_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaffa202038-45">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Santa_Croce_Firenze_Apr_2008_(17).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Santa_Croce_Firenze_Apr_2008_%2817%29.JPG/220px-Santa_Croce_Firenze_Apr_2008_%2817%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Santa_Croce_Firenze_Apr_2008_%2817%29.JPG/330px-Santa_Croce_Firenze_Apr_2008_%2817%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Santa_Croce_Firenze_Apr_2008_%2817%29.JPG/440px-Santa_Croce_Firenze_Apr_2008_%2817%29.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Santa_Croce_Firenze_Apr_2008_(17).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cenotaph" title="Cenotaph">Cenotaph</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basilica_di_Santa_Croce_di_Firenze" title="Basilica di Santa Croce di Firenze">Basilica of Santa Croce, Florence</a></div></div></div>
<p>Florence eventually came to regret having exiled Dante. The city made repeated requests for the return of his remains. The custodians of the body in Ravenna refused, at one point going so far as to conceal the bones in a false wall of the monastery. Florence built a tomb for Dante in 1829, in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basilica_di_Santa_Croce_di_Firenze" title="Basilica di Santa Croce di Firenze">Basilica of Santa Croce</a>. That <a href="/wiki/Cenotaph" title="Cenotaph">tomb has been empty</a> ever since, with Dante's body remaining in Ravenna. The front of his tomb in Florence reads <i>Onorate l'altissimo poeta</i> ‚Äî which roughly translates as "Honor the most exalted poet" and is a quote from the fourth canto of the <i>Inferno</i>.
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1945, the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Social_Republic" title="Italian Social Republic">fascist government</a> discussed bringing Dante‚Äôs remains to the <a href="/wiki/Valtellina_Redoubt" title="Valtellina Redoubt">Valtellina Redoubt</a>, the Alpine valley in which the regime intended to make its last stand against the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allies</a>. The case was made that "the greatest symbol of Italianness" should be present at fascism's "heroic" end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERaffa2020244‚Äì245_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERaffa2020244‚Äì245-47">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dante.deathmask.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="480" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Dante.deathmask.jpg/170px-Dante.deathmask.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Dante.deathmask.jpg/255px-Dante.deathmask.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Dante.deathmask.jpg/340px-Dante.deathmask.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dante.deathmask.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Recreated death mask of Dante Alighieri in Palazzo Vecchio, Florence</div></div></div>
<p>A copy of Dante's so-called death mask has been displayed since 1911 in the <a href="/wiki/Palazzo_Vecchio" title="Palazzo Vecchio">Palazzo Vecchio</a>; scholars today believe it is not a true death mask and was probably carved in 1483, perhaps by <a href="/wiki/Pietro_Lombardo" title="Pietro Lombardo">Pietro</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tullio_Lombardo" title="Tullio Lombardo">Tullio Lombardo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dante_Alighieri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2_Euro_Italia_Dante.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1645" data-file-width="1834" decoding="async" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/2_Euro_Italia_Dante.jpg/220px-2_Euro_Italia_Dante.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/2_Euro_Italia_Dante.jpg/330px-2_Euro_Italia_Dante.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/2_Euro_Italia_Dante.jpg/440px-2_Euro_Italia_Dante.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2_Euro_Italia_Dante.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dante in the national side of the Italian 2 euro coin</div></div></div>
<p>The first formal biography of Dante was the <i>Vita di Dante</i> (also known as <i>Trattatello in laude di Dante</i>), written after 1348 by <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Boccaccio" title="Giovanni Boccaccio">Giovanni Boccaccio</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup> Although several statements and episodes of it have been deemed unreliable on the basis of modern research, an earlier account of Dante's life and works had been included in the <i><a href="/wiki/Nuova_Cronica" title="Nuova Cronica">Nuova Cronica</a></i> of the Florentine chronicler <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Villani" title="Giovanni Villani">Giovanni Villani</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some 16th-century English Protestants, such as <a href="/wiki/John_Bale" title="John Bale">John Bale</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Foxe" title="John Foxe">John Foxe</a>, argued that Dante was a <a href="/wiki/Proto-Protestantism" title="Proto-Protestantism">proto-Protestant</a> because of his opposition to the pope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 19th century saw a "Dante revival", a product of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_revival" title="Medieval revival">medieval revival</a>, which was itself an important aspect of <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romanticism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Carlyle" title="Thomas Carlyle">Thomas Carlyle</a> profiled him in "The Hero as Poet", the third lecture in <i><a href="/wiki/On_Heroes,_Hero-Worship,_%26_the_Heroic_in_History" title="On Heroes, Hero-Worship, &amp; the Heroic in History">On Heroes, Hero-Worship, &amp; the Heroic in History</a></i> (1841): "He is world-great not because he is worldwide, but because he is world-deep."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[53]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Leigh_Hunt" title="Leigh Hunt">Leigh Hunt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Francis_Cary" title="Henry Francis Cary">Henry Francis Cary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Wadsworth_Longfellow" title="Henry Wadsworth Longfellow">Henry Wadsworth Longfellow</a> were among Dante's translators of the era.
</p><p>Italy's first dreadnought battleship was completed in 1913 and named <i><a href="/wiki/Italian_battleship_Dante_Alighieri" title="Italian battleship Dante Alighieri">Dante Alighieri</a></i> in honor of him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 30 April 1921, in honor of the 600th anniversary of Dante's death, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Benedict_XV" title="Pope Benedict XV">Pope Benedict XV</a> promulgated an encyclical named <i><a href="/wiki/In_praeclara_summorum" title="In praeclara summorum">In praeclara summorum</a></i>, naming Dante as one "of the many celebrated geniuses of whom the Catholic faith can boast" and the "pride and glory of humanity".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 7 December 1965, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_VI" title="Pope Paul VI">Pope Paul VI</a> promulgated the Latin <i><a href="/wiki/Motu_proprio" title="Motu proprio">motu proprio</a></i> titled <i>Altissimi cantus</i>, which was dedicated to Dante's figure and poetry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup> In that year, the pope also donated a golden iron <a href="/wiki/Crosses_in_heraldry#History" title="Crosses in heraldry">Greek Cross</a> to Dante's burial site in <a href="/wiki/Ravenna" title="Ravenna">Ravenna</a>, in occasion of the 700th anniversary of his birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup> The same cross was blessed by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Francis" title="Pope Francis">Pope Francis</a> in October 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2007, a reconstruction of Dante's face was undertaken in a collaborative project. Artists from Pisa University and forensic engineers at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bologna" title="University of Bologna">University of Bologna</a> at <a href="/wiki/Forl%C3%AC" title="Forl√¨">Forl√¨</a> constructed the model, portraying Dante's features as somewhat different from what was once thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nosejob_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-nosejob-62">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2008, the Municipality of Florence officially apologized for expelling Dante 700 years earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>A celebration was held in 2015 at Italy's <a href="/wiki/Senate_of_the_Republic_(Italy)" title="Senate of the Republic (Italy)">Senate of the Republic</a> for the 750th anniversary of Dante's birth. It included a commemoration from <a href="/wiki/Pope_Francis" title="Pope Francis">Pope Francis</a>, who also issued the apostolic letter <i><a href="/wiki/Cando_lucis_aeternae" title="Cando lucis aeternae">Cando lucis aeternae</a></i> in honor of the anniversary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 2021, a symbolic re-trial of Dante Alighieri was held virtually in Florence to posthumously clear his name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Works">Works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dante_Alighieri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Works_by_Dante_Alighieri" title="Category:Works by Dante Alighieri">Category:Works by Dante Alighieri</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alighieri_-_Divina_Commedia,_Nel_mille_quatro_cento_septe_et_due_nel_quarto_mese_adi_cinque_et_sei_-_2384293_id00022000_Scan00006.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3337" data-file-width="2248" decoding="async" height="252" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Alighieri_-_Divina_Commedia%2C_Nel_mille_quatro_cento_septe_et_due_nel_quarto_mese_adi_cinque_et_sei_-_2384293_id00022000_Scan00006.jpg/170px-Alighieri_-_Divina_Commedia%2C_Nel_mille_quatro_cento_septe_et_due_nel_quarto_mese_adi_cinque_et_sei_-_2384293_id00022000_Scan00006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Alighieri_-_Divina_Commedia%2C_Nel_mille_quatro_cento_septe_et_due_nel_quarto_mese_adi_cinque_et_sei_-_2384293_id00022000_Scan00006.jpg/255px-Alighieri_-_Divina_Commedia%2C_Nel_mille_quatro_cento_septe_et_due_nel_quarto_mese_adi_cinque_et_sei_-_2384293_id00022000_Scan00006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Alighieri_-_Divina_Commedia%2C_Nel_mille_quatro_cento_septe_et_due_nel_quarto_mese_adi_cinque_et_sei_-_2384293_id00022000_Scan00006.jpg/340px-Alighieri_-_Divina_Commedia%2C_Nel_mille_quatro_cento_septe_et_due_nel_quarto_mese_adi_cinque_et_sei_-_2384293_id00022000_Scan00006.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alighieri_-_Divina_Commedia,_Nel_mille_quatro_cento_septe_et_due_nel_quarto_mese_adi_cinque_et_sei_-_2384293_id00022000_Scan00006.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Divina Commedia</i> (1472)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DanteDetail.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="684" data-file-width="1053" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/DanteDetail.jpg/220px-DanteDetail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/DanteDetail.jpg/330px-DanteDetail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/DanteDetail.jpg/440px-DanteDetail.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DanteDetail.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dante, poised between the mountain of purgatory and the city of Florence, displays the <a href="/wiki/Incipit" title="Incipit">incipit</a> <i>Nel mezzo del cammin di nostra vita</i> in a detail of <a href="/wiki/Domenico_di_Michelino" title="Domenico di Michelino">Domenico di Michelino</a>'s painting, Florence, 1465</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dante_Alighieri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most of Dante's literary work was composed after his exile in 1301. <i><a href="/wiki/La_Vita_Nuova" title="La Vita Nuova">La Vita Nuova</a></i> ("The New Life") is the only major work that predates it; it is a collection of lyric poems (sonnets and songs) with commentary in prose, ostensibly intended to be circulated in manuscript form, as was customary for such poems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[69]</a></sup> It also contains, or constructs, the story of his love for <a href="/wiki/Beatrice_Portinari" title="Beatrice Portinari">Beatrice Portinari</a>, who later served as the ultimate symbol of salvation in the <i>Comedy</i>, a function already indicated in the final pages of the <i>Vita Nuova</i>. The work contains many of Dante's love poems in Tuscan, which was not unprecedented; the vernacular had been regularly used for lyric works before, during all the thirteenth century. However, Dante's commentary on his own work is also in the vernacular‚Äîboth in the <i>Vita Nuova</i> and in the <i>Convivio</i>‚Äîinstead of the Latin that was almost universally used.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Divine_Comedy" title="Divine Comedy">Divine Comedy</a></i> describes Dante's journey through <a href="/wiki/Divine_Comedy#Inferno" title="Divine Comedy">Hell</a> (<i>Inferno</i>), <a href="/wiki/Divine_Comedy#Purgatorio" title="Divine Comedy">Purgatory</a> (<i>Purgatorio</i>), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/9_spheres_of_heaven" title="9 spheres of heaven">Paradise</a> (<i>Paradiso</i>); he is first guided by the Roman poet <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a> and then by <a href="/wiki/Beatrice_Portinari" title="Beatrice Portinari">Beatrice</a>. Of the books, <i>Purgatorio</i> is arguably the most lyrical of the three, referring to more contemporary poets and artists than <i>Inferno</i>; <i>Paradiso</i> is the most heavily theological, and the one in which, many scholars have argued, the <i>Divine Comedy'</i>s most beautiful and mystic passages appear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>With its seriousness of purpose, its literary stature and the range‚Äîboth stylistic and thematic‚Äîof its content, the <i>Comedy</i> soon became a cornerstone in the evolution of Italian as an established literary language. Dante was more aware than most early Italian writers of the variety of Italian dialects and of the need to create a literature and a unified literary language beyond the limits of Latin writing at the time; in that sense, he is a forerunner of the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, with its effort to create vernacular literature in competition with earlier classical writers. Dante's in-depth knowledge (within the limits of his time) of Roman antiquity, and his evident admiration for some aspects of pagan Rome, also point forward to the 15th century. Ironically, while he was widely honored in the centuries after his death, the <i>Comedy</i> slipped out of fashion among men of letters: too medieval, too rough and tragic, and not stylistically refined in the respects that the high and late Renaissance came to demand of literature.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dante_Luca.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1012" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Dante_Luca.jpg/220px-Dante_Luca.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Dante_Luca.jpg/330px-Dante_Luca.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Dante_Luca.jpg/440px-Dante_Luca.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dante_Luca.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dante Alighieri, detail from <a href="/wiki/Luca_Signorelli" title="Luca Signorelli">Luca Signorelli</a>'s fresco in the Chapel of San Brizio, <a href="/wiki/Orvieto_Cathedral" title="Orvieto Cathedral">Orvieto Cathedral</a></div></div></div>
<p>He wrote the <i>Comedy</i> in a language he called "Italian", in some sense an amalgamated literary language mostly based on the regional dialect of Tuscany, but with some elements of Latin and other regional dialects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-74">[72]</a></sup> He deliberately aimed to reach a readership throughout Italy including laymen, clergymen and other poets. By creating a poem of epic structure and philosophic purpose, he established that the Italian language was suitable for the highest sort of expression. In French, Italian is sometimes nicknamed <i>la langue de Dante</i>. Publishing in the vernacular language marked Dante as one of the first in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> Western Europe (among others such as <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">Geoffrey Chaucer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Boccaccio" title="Giovanni Boccaccio">Giovanni Boccaccio</a>) to break free from standards of publishing in only Latin (the language of <a href="/wiki/Catholic_liturgy" title="Catholic liturgy">liturgy</a>, history and scholarship in general, but often also of lyric poetry). This break set a precedent and allowed more literature to be published for a wider audience, setting the stage for greater levels of literacy in the future. However, unlike Boccaccio, <a href="/wiki/John_Milton" title="John Milton">Milton</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ludovico_Ariosto" title="Ludovico Ariosto">Ariosto</a>, Dante did not really become an author read across Europe until the Romantic era. To the Romantics, Dante, like <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">Shakespeare</a>, was a prime example of the "original genius" who set his own rules, created persons of overpowering stature and depth, and went far beyond any imitation of the patterns of earlier masters; and who, in turn, could not truly be imitated.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Throughout the 19th century, Dante's reputation grew and solidified; and by 1865, the 600th anniversary of his birth, he had become established as one of the greatest literary icons of the Western world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>New readers often wonder how such a serious work may be called a "comedy". In the <a href="/wiki/Comedy#Etymology" title="Comedy">classical sense</a> the word <i>comedy</i> refers to works that reflect belief in an ordered universe, in which events tend toward not only a happy or amusing ending but one influenced by a Providential will that orders all things to an ultimate good. By this meaning of the word, as Dante himself allegedly wrote in a letter to <a href="/wiki/Cangrande_I_della_Scala" title="Cangrande I della Scala">Cangrande I della Scala</a>, the progression of the pilgrimage from Hell to Paradise is the paradigmatic expression of comedy, since the work begins with the pilgrim's moral confusion and ends with the vision of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>A number of other works are credited to Dante. <i><a href="/wiki/Convivio" title="Convivio">Convivio</a></i> ("The Banquet")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup> is a collection of his longest poems with an (unfinished) allegorical commentary. <i><a href="/wiki/De_Monarchia" title="De Monarchia">De Monarchia</a></i> ("On Monarchy")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[76]</a></sup> is a summary treatise of political philosophy in Latin which was condemned and burned after Dante's death<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup> by the Papal Legate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bertrando_del_Poggetto" title="Bertrando del Poggetto">Bertrando del Poggetto</a>, which argues for the necessity of a universal or global monarchy to establish universal peace in this life, and this monarchy's relationship to the Roman Catholic Church as guide to eternal peace. <i><a href="/wiki/De_vulgari_eloquentia" title="De vulgari eloquentia">De vulgari eloquentia</a></i> ("On the Eloquence in the Vernacular")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup> is a treatise on vernacular literature, partly inspired by the <i>Razos de trobar</i> of <a href="/wiki/Raimon_Vidal_de_Bezaudun" title="Raimon Vidal de Bezaudun">Raimon Vidal de Bezaudun</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup> <i>Quaestio de aqua et terra</i> ("A Question of the Water and of the Land") is a theological work discussing the arrangement of Earth's dry land and ocean. The <i><a href="/wiki/Eclogues_(Dante)" title="Eclogues (Dante)">Eclogues</a></i> are two poems addressed to the poet Giovanni del Virgilio. Dante is also sometimes credited with writing <i>Il Fiore</i> ("The Flower"), a series of sonnets summarizing <i><a href="/wiki/Roman_de_la_Rose" title="Roman de la Rose">Le Roman de la Rose</a></i>, and <i>Detto d'Amore</i> ("Tale of Love"), a short narrative poem also based on <i>Le Roman de la Rose</i>. These would be the earliest, and most novice, of his known works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Le_Rime" title="Le Rime">Le Rime</a></i> is a posthumous collection of miscellaneous poems.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_works">List of works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dante_Alighieri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: List of works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The major works of Dante's are the following.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i>Il Fiore</i> and <i>Detto d'Amore</i> ("The Flower" and "Tale of Love", 1283‚Äì7)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/La_Vita_Nuova" title="La Vita Nuova">La Vita Nuova</a></i> ("The New Life", 1294)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/De_vulgari_eloquentia" title="De vulgari eloquentia">De vulgari eloquentia</a></i> ("On the Eloquence in the Vernacular", 1302‚Äì5)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Convivio" title="Convivio">Convivio</a></i> ("The Banquet", 1307)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/De_Monarchia" title="De Monarchia">De Monarchia</a></i> ("On Monarchy", 1313)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Divine_Comedy" title="Divine Comedy">Divina Commedia</a></i> ("Divine Comedy", 1320)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Eclogues_(Dante)" title="Eclogues (Dante)">Eclogues</a></i> (1320)</li>
<li><i>Quaestio de aqua et terra</i> ("A Question of the Water and of the Land", 1320)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Le_Rime" title="Le Rime">Le Rime</a></i></li></ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dante_Alighieri&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The name 'Dante' is understood to be a <a href="/wiki/Hypocorism" title="Hypocorism">hypocorism</a> of the name 'Durante', though no document known to survive from Dante's lifetime refers to him as such (including his own writings). A document prepared for Dante's son Jacopo refers to "Durante, often called Dante". He may have been named for his maternal grandfather Durante degli Abati.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1011085734" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Though an <a href="/wiki/Unification_of_Italy" title="Unification of Italy">Italian nation state</a> had yet to be established, the Latin equivalent of the <a href="/wiki/Italians#Name" title="Italians">term Italian</a> had been in use for natives of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_geographical_region" title="Italian geographical region">the region</a> since antiquity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

Istituto dell'Enciclopedia Italiana.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Alighieri%2C+Dante&amp;rft.btitle=Dizionario+Biografico+degli+Italiani&amp;rft.pub=Istituto+dell%27Enciclopedia+Italiana&amp;rft.date=1960&amp;rft.aulast=Chimenz&amp;rft.aufirst=Siro+A.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.treccani.it%2Fenciclopedia%2Fdante-alighieri_%2528Dizionario-Biografico%2529%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADante+Alighieri"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">His birth date is listed as "probably in the end of May" by Robert Hollander in "Dante" in <i><a href="/wiki/Dictionary_of_the_Middle_Ages" title="Dictionary of the Middle Ages">Dictionary of the Middle Ages</a>,</i> volume ¬†4. According to <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Boccaccio" title="Giovanni Boccaccio">Giovanni Boccaccio</a>, the poet said he was born in May. See "Alighieri, Dante" in the <i>Dizionario Biografico degli Italiani.</i></span>
</li>
